CNET","url":{"href":"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.cnet.com","rel":"alternate","type":"text\u002Fhtml"}},"image_sizes":[{"type":"ORIG","sizes":[{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F3ac541ca582bee49f458cc80fd3326b67b5342f3.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":500,"height":281,"size_type":"original","formats":{}}],"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002Foriginal\u002FG5RkScNh26KnR9I8fMUJ91Yco5w"},{"type":"ORIG","sizes":[{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F34796c7125aca3cdaa9a7667dfa66b7cf2c6e6a1.png","type":"image\u002Fpng","width":500,"height":340,"size_type":"original","formats":{}}],"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002Foriginal\u002F8OzCqWAOKNGXPl2rJZy4KoRxnVk"}],"highlight_color":"#ffffff","data_version":"1","title":"Microsoft Just Made Its Office Mobile Apps Free to Use Without Subscription","summary":"The software giant on Thursday unveiled a handful of changes to its Office mobile software on smartphones and tablets running Apple’s and Google’s operating systems, refreshing its Word, PowerPoint, and Excel apps to make them friendlier for the mobile age.","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"Microsoft Just Made Its Office Mobile Apps Free to Use Without Subscription\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F3ac541ca582bee49f458cc80fd3326b67b5342f3.jpg\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003EMobile customers want robust, free software that works with a lot of apps and can be accessed anywhere. So far, Microsoft has failed to embrace those concepts &#x2014; but it&#x2019;s working to change that.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThe software giant on Thursday unveiled&#xA0;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.cnet.com\u002Fnews\u002Foffice-for-ios-goes-freemium-what-you-need-to-know\u002F\"\u003Ea handful of changes to its Office mobile software\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#xA0;on smartphones and&#xA0;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.cnet.com\u002Ftopics\u002Ftablets\u002F\"\u003Etablets\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#xA0;running Apple&#x2019;s and Google&#x2019;s operating systems, refreshing its Word, PowerPoint, and Excel apps to make them friendlier for the mobile age.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThe most notable change is that users on Apple&#x2019;s iPhone, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.cnet.com\u002Fproducts\u002Fapple-ipad-air\u002F\"\u003EiPad\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#xA0;tablet, or devices running Google&#x2019;s&#xA0;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.cnet.com\u002Fandroid-update\u002F\"\u003EAndroid\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#xA0;operating system will be able to create and edit Office content for free. Before, doing that required an Office 365 subscription.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EMicrosoft is the latest company to embrace the concept of &#x201C;freemium,&#x201D; in which many core aspects of a game or app are given away, but there exists an option to pay up for additional bells and whistles. The shift represents Microsoft&#x2019;s revamped strategy of going after as broad a market as possible.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;We want more people to use our applications,&#x201D; Amanda Lefebvre, an Office product manager, said in an interview. &#x201C;Usage is a primary goal for us. We want to give them more reasons to use our product.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAs part of the changes, Microsoft also decided to blow up Office for iPhone, which included basic versions of Word, PowerPoint, and Excel all in one app. Instead, it&#x2019;s now providing three standalone apps of Word, PowerPoint, and Excel with more capabilities, just like the company did with its Office for iPad apps.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"image\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F34796c7125aca3cdaa9a7667dfa66b7cf2c6e6a1.png\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003EThese changes come just two days after Microsoft and cloud-storage company Dropbox&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.cnet.com\u002Fnews\u002Fmicrosoft-dropbox-partner-up-in-mobile-move\u002F\"\u003Eunveiled a collaboration\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E&#xA0;to allow users to access Dropbox directly from Office apps and edit Office files from the Dropbox app.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWhile the Office suite of word-processing, spreadsheet, and presentation software have dominated in the PC world, Microsoft has had little success utilizing that software to draw customers to its Windows Phone operating system on mobile devices. These days, Apple&#x2019;s iOS and Google&#x2019;s Android operating systems control nearly all the smartphone and tablet market, with Microsoft taking just 2.5 percent of the market, according to researcher IDC.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EUnder CEO Satya Nadella, who took over in February, Microsoft is taking a different tack, trying to make Office more open on other operating systems to increase its user base and hopefully convert more users to premium subscriptions, which include a handful of more powerful tools on the mobile apps. The changes may also help Microsoft draw some customers away from Google&#x2019;s productivity tools, which many users can access for free.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;We want to be where our customers are, and we want to make sure that we have a caveat-free commitment to phones and tablets,&#x201D; Lefebvre said.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWith the premium versions of Office apps, customers get more editing tools in Word, such as custom color changes of text and more precise controls for editing charts and tablets. Also, paying Word users can see documents in both portrait and landscape modes, while free users can in most cases only see documents in portrait. For PowerPoint, the paid version comes with &#x201C;Presenter View,&#x201D; which lets users see live notes and a thumbnail strip of the upcoming slides.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAs part of the new changes, the iPad apps were updated and will be available starting Thursday, along with the iPhone apps. The Office apps on the iPad tablet have seen some success, with over 40 million downloads so far. Microsoft didn&#x2019;t disclose the number of iPhone downloads.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EApps for Android tablets can be previewed by customers if they sign up starting Thursday, but general availability of the new software isn&#x2019;t expected until early 2015.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ch6\u003E\u003Cem\u003ERELATED STORIES\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fh6\u003E \n\u003Cul\u003E\n three faves. (Courtesy of Brooklyn Reiff)\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBy Monday morning this week, an otherwise normal kid who worked at Target discovered that he had become an \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ftech\u002Fwho-is-alex-from-target-and-why-should-i-even-begin-101686693754.html\"\u003Eunwitting Internet meme\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, amassing thousands of new Twitter followers and sparking coverage from every major media outlet (including Yahoo Tech). By Tuesday, a previously unknown company \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ftech\u002Falex-from-target-may-actually-be-a-marketing-campaign-101799506799.html\"\u003Etried to piggyback on his viral-ness\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. As the week wore on, a slew of think pieces came out about \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.buzzfeed.com\u002Fryanhatesthis\u002Fthe-parody-twitter-illuminati\"\u003Efandom\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fnymag.com\u002Fthecut\u002F2014\u002F11\u002Falex-from-target-fame.html\"\u003Emodern fame\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThe source of all this madness: a 15-year-old high-schooler named \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002Fbrooklynjreiff\"\u003EBrooklyn Reiff\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"Brooklyn Reiff\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F57c24c5f4a314ba1fb71e2f42adeaf7faabddf12.jpg\"\u003E\u003Cem\u003EBrooklyn Reiff. (Courtesy of Reiff)\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EReiff is a junior at Prosper High School in Prosper, Texas. She&#x2019;s on the softball team there, where she plays left field, sometimes center. src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002Fc0f958011c7161670d6349f9f5e206757db33233.jpg\"\u003E\u003Cem\u003E(BGR)\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EFoxconn, the Chinese manufacturing giant where the world&#x2019;s iPhones are assembled, has a \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.nytimes.com\u002F2012\u002F01\u002F26\u002Fbusiness\u002Fieconomy-apples-ipad-and-the-human-costs-for-workers-in-china.html\"\u003Ereputation\u003C\u002Fa\u003E for poor working conditions. Now a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Flibcom.org\u002Fblog\u002Fxulizhi-foxconn-suicide-poetry\"\u003Etrove of translated poetry\u003C\u002Fa\u003E from a factory worker who committed suicide shines new light into the devastating emotional and physical toll of that job.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003EXu Lizhi first began working in the assembly line at Foxconn&#x2019;s Shenzen facility in 2010. During his employment, he published nearly 30 pieces in the company&#x2019;s internal newspaper, &#x201C;Foxconn People,&#x201D; including poems describing the plant&#x2019;s poor working conditions. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003ELizhi killed himself in September 2014 at the age of 24. In late October, the \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Flibcom.org\u002Fblog\u002Fxulizhi-foxconn-suicide-poetry\"\u003EN&#xE0;o Project\u003C\u002Fa\u003E &#x2014; a multilingual blog and journal dedicated to human rights &#x2014; \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.businessinsider.com\u002Ffoxconn-factory-workers-suicide-poems-2014-11\"\u003Etranslated his obituary and poems\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to &#x201C;\u003Cspan\u003Espread awareness [of] the harsh conditions, struggles and aspirations of Chinese migrant workers.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg alt=\"Obituary in Shenzen Evening News\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w432\u002Ffe64804e8579b9f56da20cf8cbbf27891f6fb0df.png\"\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cem\u003EA screenshot of Lizhi&#x2019;s obituary, which ran in his hometown paper, the&#xA0;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwb.sznews.com\u002Fhtml\u002F2014-10\u002F10\u002Fcontent_3025410.htm\"\u003EShenzhen Evening News\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003EThe titles of Lizhi&#x2019;s work include&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E&#x201C;I Fall Asleep, Just Standing Like That&#x201D; and &#x201C;The Last Graveyard.&#x201D; They depict a joyless, almost robotic existence full of overnight shifts, docile suffering, and a toxic working environment.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E\u003Cspan\u003EOne of Lizhi&#x2019;s shorter works from January 2014,&#xA0;\u003Cspan\u003E&#x201C;A Screw Fell to the Ground,&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E hints at the company&#x2019;s lack of concern for exhausted employees.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EA screw fell to the ground\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Cem\u003EIn this dark night of overtime\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Cem\u003EPlunging vertically, lightly clinking\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Cem\u003EIt won&#x2019;t attract anyone&#x2019;s attention\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Cem\u003EJust like last time\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Cem\u003EOn a night like this\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Cem\u003EWhen someone plunged to the ground\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ENews of Foxconn&#x2019;s inhumane conditions spread to international circles as early as 2010, the year that 18 Foxconn workers separately \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.reuters.com\u002Farticle\u002F2010\u002F11\u002F05\u002Fus-china-foxconn-death-idUSTRE6A41M920101105\"\u003Eattempted suicide\u003C\u002Fa\u003E by leaping off a structure at the manufacturing facility. Fourteen died. The pattern \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fonline.wsj.com\u002Fnews\u002Farticles\u002FSB10001424052748704026204575267603576594936?mg=reno64-wsj&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fonline.wsj.com%2Farticle%2FSB10001424052748704026204575267603576594936.html\"\u003Esparked inquiries\u003C\u002Fa\u003E from some of Foxconn&#x2019;s customers, including Apple and Hewlett-Packard.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBy 2012, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.nytimes.com\u002F2012\u002F12\u002F27\u002Fbusiness\u002Fsigns-of-changes-taking-hold-in-electronics-factories-in-china.html\"\u003EThe New York Times\u003C\u002Fa\u003E reported that Foxconn had improved its conditions, after company executives sat down with Apple officials and negotiated changes. Employees were given chairs to sit on (which previously were considered incentives for laziness), as well as more substantial improvements such as shorter shifts and higher pay.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003EBut the fact that these disturbing poems appeared in an internal factory newspaper &#x2014; and that not one Foxconn supervisor, administrator, or executive expressed concern for Lizhi or his co-workers &#x2014; is a telling sign that the company is far from resolving the problems that have plagued it in the past.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E
